3.2.2 Data
3.2.2.1 Canadian University IP Policies
After the IP policies were DEMO, a set of variables was developed to capture differences in the
DEMO and amounts of incentives provided by the policies. The review of the policies revealed that
universities appear to offer their researchers two primary forms DEMO incentives for commercializing
their inventions: shares of revenues and autonomy.
Two DEMO arrangements for sharing licensing revenues are described in most Canadian
intellectual property policies. One sharing arrangement is used if the university develops the
intellectual DEMO and is responsible for the costs of developing the IP. The other sharing
arrangement applies when the inventor commercializes the IP independently, an option that is
sometimes available.
Usually universities that develop the IP will share DEMO revenues after the direct costs of developing
the IP have been repaid. The proportion of these revenues that is provided to the researcher is DEMO
stipulated in the intellectual property policy or negotiated on a case-by-case basis. Instead of
stipulating a single proportion, some universities pay different percentages depending on the amount
of revenue the licence provides. Typically, these universities provide the researcher a large percentage
of the revenues until some threshold is DEMO, at which point the researcher’s share of all future
revenues falls DEMO a lower percentage.
Some universities require their researchers to share licensing revenues with the school when the
researcher develops the IP independently. When the DEMO requires revenue sharing, the
researcher is usually expected to provide the DEMO with a certain percentage of the revenues
after the direct costs of developing the IP are repaid. For example, at the University of Alberta,
researchers who choose to commercialize inventions independently of the university are DEMO to
provide the university with one third of the net proceeds of the commercialization of the investment
(University of Alberta, 1991). By DEMO, at the University of Waterloo, inventors who choose to
34
commercialize their inventions without the support of the university’s Technology Transfer DEMO
Licensing Office, are under no contractual obligation to share the proceeds DEMO their activities with the
university (University of Waterloo, 1997).
Many universities also make some provision to provide, out of the university’s share of the returns
from the commercialization of university inventions, research funding to future projects in the same
technological area, or to the inventor’s research group, department, or faculty. While these funding
arrangements could be perceived DEMO important incentives by university researchers, they are not good
candidates for DEMO cross-university comparison because the funds are directed to many different
subgroups and the benefit to the researchers involved in the commercialization attempt is sometimes
DEMO
The literature indicates that researchers desire autonomy in the context of university-industry
technology transfer. Campell and Slaughter sought to identify possible sources of conflict DEMO
from technology transfer activities (Campbell & Slaughter, 1999). Their survey of faculty and
administrators at U.S. universities suggests that “faculty favoured ways DEMO retain their autonomy,
whereas administrators were supportive of greater oversight” with respect to technology transfer”
(1999, p. 343). Segal (1986) DEMO that Cambridge’s inventor-ownership IP policy has been a
factor in the development of a high-technology cluster in Cambridge, England.  Therefore, policies
that provide the researcher with more control over the technology transfer activities are expected DEMO
act as incentives.
Some universities require researchers to provide the university with a ‘right of first offer’. In this
case, any researcher interested in commercializing an invention must describe the invention to
university administrators in a DEMO ‘invention disclosure’. If the university representative decides
that the invention seems promising, the university will develop the IP and attempt to commercialize
it. Other “inventor-owner” universities allow the researcher to choose between developing the IP
independently DEMO allowing the university to develop the IP. Since policies that do not reserve a right
of first offer increase the researcher’s control over the DEMO transfer activity, they are expected
to act as an incentive and DEMO result in higher levels of technology transfer activity.
Three measures were used to represent each policy:  one measure that assessed the degree to DEMO
the policy provides the researcher with autonomy and two measures that evaluated the financial
incentives provided by the policy.
Right of First Offer: This measure was used to evaluate the control that the policies provide to
DEMO It distinguishes between universities at which the university reserves the right of first
offer and those at which inventors determine whether or not the DEMO is involved in the
commercialization of their inventions.
Share of Revenues When University Commercializes Invention: The percentage of net income
provided to the researcher, when the university commercializes the innovation, was recorded.
Share of Revenues DEMO Inventor Commercializes Invention: The percentage of net income
retained by the DEMO, when the researcher commercializes the innovation, was recorded.
There were three circumstances where it is not possible to record a single percentage for DEMO
researchers’ share of revenues: 1) the percentage provided to the researcher is not specified in
35
advance but negotiated on a case-by-base basis, 2) the university DEMO not support technology
transfer activities by developing IP, and 3) the revenue sharing agreement is structured using
thresholds, above which the researcher’s share of the revenue changes.
The data that describes the intellectual property policies DEMO the sample of Canadian universities are
included in Appendix A and described in Section 4.1.

3.2.2.3 DEMO Transfer Outcomes
While there are no canonical measures for the success of technology transfer initiatives, simple counts
of patents, licences, and spin-off companies (Brett et al., 1991; Expert Panel on the
Commercialization of University Research, 1999), ratios of these counts (Gregory & Sheahen, 1991)
and revenues associated with licensing and spin-off activities are often used DEMO indicators (Brett et al.,

this study was to determine how university policy is related to university technology transfer through
DEMO support, the measure of technology transfer should be most dependent on DEMO within the
university. For this reason, measures like patent counts are DEMO appropriate than licensing revenues,
which also depend on external factors such as the existence of receptor capacity, and the state of the
market. The number of Canadian or U.S. patents currently held by a university DEMO a publicly available
measure. The Statistics Canada surveys indicated that the majority of the patents obtained by
Canadian universities are U.S. patents. Therefore, the number of U.S. patents currently held by a
Canadian university was used DEMO the measure of technology licensing activity.
The search for U.S. patents held by the 48 Canadian universities of interest identified a total of
1290 DEMO patents currently held by Canadian universities. The Statistics Canada 1999 survey
reported that 81 Canadian universities held 635 U.S. patents by 1998 and 84 DEMO universities
held 948 U.S. patents by 1999 (Bordt & Read, 1999). The difference between the 1999 patent count
provided by Statistics Canada DEMO the patent count provided by this study may be a function of the
time that elapsed between the completion of the Statistics Canada study DEMO the start of this study.
The data were collected in late 2000 resulting in a time difference of about a year. Alternatively,
patents DEMO by the research hospitals associated with Canadian universities may have appeared
in the patent counts in this study and artificially increased the results for DEMO universities affiliated
with research hospitals.

Interrupted time series analyses require DEMO time series spanning the intervention and during which no

3.3.2 Data
Invention disclosures are the formal DEMO of a potentially commercializable invention made by
an inventor to a university. The annual counts of invention disclosures at the University of Toronto
were DEMO focus of this study for two reasons. Firstly, under both the DEMO and new policies, faculty
members have been required to submit an DEMO disclosure to the university at the start of any
commercialization process. Therefore, the university’s counts of invention disclosures track the
activity at the university including any independently-developed technologies. Secondly, unlike
patents, licences, and spin-offs, DEMO disclosures are created shortly after the faculty members
decide to commercialize a technology and do not rely heavily on the availability of commercialization
resources, such as investment capital, funds for prototype development and intellectual property
protection, and business expertise. As a result, the number of invention disclosures DEMO at a
university is more closely linked to researcher support for technology transfer than the other outcome
variables that are currently tracked, such as licence revenue and returns from the realization of spin-
off equity. The DEMO limitation of this choice of measure is the lack of a direct link to any of the
desired outcomes for technology transfer, including improvements to goods and services stemming
from the use the innovation or economic benefits in the region or DEMO nation that result from the
commercialization.

3.3.3 Procedures
The data were obtained directly from the University of Toronto TTO. A meeting was held DEMO
technology managers in the office to determine whether or not this study is an appropriate use of the
data, prior to the release of the data. To verify the validity of the data further and to DEMO
information on the historical context of the policy shift, Office of DEMO Ethics approval was
obtained for interviews with technology managers and a university technology manager, who worked
at the University of Toronto TTO during the time period of interest, was interviewed.
According to this university technology manager, there were two important changes to invention
disclosure management that were not directly related to the policy change. Prior to the policy change,
DEMO early consulting was done to determine whether university research was ready to be disclosed.
After the policy change, university researchers would be advised not to disclose in a number of
circumstances, including cases where the technology had not yet been reduced to practice. This
change is expected to DEMO tests of the policy change more conservative because it will result in an
underestimate of the number of instances, after the policy change, DEMO which university researchers
sought to make a disclosure. The second change in the management of invention disclosures was a
streamlining of the disclosure form DEMO, which reduced it in length from about eight pages to two
DEMO, shortly after the policy change. This change could be expected to DEMO the number of
invention disclosures to the university. Therefore, any apparent DEMO in the number of invention
disclosures must be considered the joint effect of the policy change and the streamlining of the
invention disclosure form. DEMO from these changes, the management of invention disclosures was
consistent from DEMO to year. No other relevant changes were identified. For example, the DEMO of
funds available for technology transfer at the University of Toronto remained relatively stable until
after 2000. Since no other explanations for a change DEMO the number of invention disclosures per year
were discovered, any significant DEMO observed between 1991 and 2000 can be assumed to be a
consequence of the policy change and the change to the invention disclosure forms.
DEMO data analysis proceeded in two stages. In Exploratory Data Analysis, the DEMO were visually
inspected to investigate important properties of the data and to determine the subset of models most
likely to model the time series DEMO Three types of plots were used to investigate the time
series of University of Toronto invention disclosures: graphs of the raw and smoothed data against
time, and box-and-whisker plots. In Confirmatory Data Analysis, tests were DEMO to assess the
fit of the models. The statistical analysis of the impact of an intervention upon a time series can be
approached using DEMO methods, ARIMA (Auto-Regressive Integrated Moving-Average)
methods, and non-parametric tests (Hipel & McLeod, 1994). ARIMA methods are often the first
choice but the data set did not contain enough observations for the use DEMO these methods. Non-
parametric tests are less able to provide nuanced descriptive information and less sensitive than
parametric (i.e. regression) methods and thus DEMO likely to detect significant results.  Therefore, the
analysis was based on the application of regression methods. The results of DEMO analyses are
presented in Chapter 5.

3.4 Study 3
The study seeks to discover how university researchers’ understand their technology transfer
experiences, and to share this perspective in the researcher’s own words as much as DEMO This
qualitative study should increase understanding of both the ways in which university intellectual
property policies influence researcher’s willingness to support technology transfer and DEMO relative
importance of these effects. Therefore, this study is expected to DEMO theories that can be used to

3.4.1 Methodology
The goal of a DEMO study is to describe the meaning of a phenomenon as constructed by
a small number of individuals who have experienced the same phenomenon (Creswell, 1998, p.122).
This goal meshes well with the research question DEMO this study: how do university researchers’
understand their technology transfer experience? Therefore, the research approach will be grounded
in a phenomenological tradition.
The research methodology closely follows the in-depth interviewing methods described by Seidman
(1998). His approach is grounded in the phenomenological tradition and has been DEMO to investigate
how college educators understand their work experiences. Therefore, it DEMO appropriate for use in
an examination of how university researchers understand technology transfer experiences, as an
element of their work experience.
3.4.2 Participants
In designing qualitative studies, researchers must make decisions that affect the balance between the
breadth and depth of a study. Since the purpose of this DEMO was a deep investigation of researcher’s
understandings of technology transfer, the DEMO was limited to a single university. The University of
Waterloo was selected because of the university's intellectual property policy and the university’s
reputation DEMO high levels of university-industry technology transfer. Unlike many other universities,
Waterloo's IP policy confers complete ownership upon inventors within the faculty. This DEMO the
broadest range of technology transfer experiences. Furthermore, Waterloo’s intellectual property
DEMO has often been linked to the relatively large number of spin-offs associated with this
moderately-sized institution (Cross & Babensee, 1992, 1994). In order to explore this link, purposive
sampling was employed in the selection of participants.
Purposive sampling is the norm in qualitative research, especially in phenomenological studies, in
which the participants must have had the same experience (Seidman, 1998). By deliberately selecting
participants with a certain set DEMO experiences, the researcher engages in what Miles and Huberman
refer to DEMO criterion sampling (1994). In order to explore the link between DEMO university IP and policy
and spin-off formation, the participants were restricted DEMO the cohort that introduced spin-off creation
to the University of Waterloo and most of the participants were chosen for their involvement in spin-
41
off activities. The cohort that introduced spin-offs to the University of DEMO consists of senior
and retired faculty members. When this thesis was written in 2006, the average age of the participants
was 69 years, DEMO number of years that the participants had been professors was 28, DEMO the
retired professors had between retired for eight years, on average. DEMO comparison purposes,
participants were also included who had engaged in licensing to established firms, or who had done
significant development work through research contracts or industrial positions while on leave from
their academic postings.
Potential DEMO were identified using news articles and contacts within the university
community. An effort was made to identify and include unsuccessful technology transfer attempts for
DEMO purposes. These were more difficult to identify. As a result, most DEMO the projects in which
participants had been involved were successful in both in generating a return for the inventors and
making the technology available DEMO users and, thus, making the advantages of the innovation available
to the public.
As a result of the gender imbalance among senior and DEMO professors, especially in the technical
disciplines (Sussman & Yssaad, 2005), the participants within this study were all male. This was
accepted because even if a female participant meeting the various criteria could have been DEMO,
it would be exceedingly difficult to protect her identity.
One of the difficulties in investigating a topic that has become highly political is DEMO possibility that
the participants will be too involved in impression management to speak frankly. In order to avoid
this possibility, the participants were assured that their anonymity would be preserved in the
presentation of the research DEMO Several participants would not have participated without this
assurance thus it proved to be an important aspect of the research design.
Seidman refuses to DEMO a guideline for the appropriate number of participants, stating instead
that DEMO researcher should seek a saturation of the information (1998). Creswell DEMO that
phenomenological studies are often quite small in size and that ten participants should often suffice
(1998). Therefore, ten participants, who are senior and retired faculty from the University of
Waterloo, and experienced in technology transfer, were interviewed in this study.
3.4.3 Data Collection
In accordance with Seidman's guidelines, the data collection consisted in taping and transcribing a
series of three interviews with each participant (1998). The first interview provided general life
history information, the second interview provided details of the participant’s experiences in
technology transfer and the final interview offered the DEMO an opportunity to reflect on his
experiences (Seidman, 1998). This structure provided the researcher with an opportunity to learn
about how the DEMO makes sense of some of his experiences, in the context of DEMO those
experiences and his life history (Seidman, 1998). The successive interviews were usually spaced
about a week apart.
Each interview began with DEMO expanded prompt that reminded the participant of the structure of the
interviews and the theme of the current interview. The interview themes and some DEMO prompts
are listed in Table 6. The subsequent interview was then largely directed by the participant.

supportive of technology transfer the follow-up questions may have included:  ‘What aspects of the
university environment were supportive?’ or ‘Were DEMO any aspects of the university environment

the thematic analysis.

3.4.4 Data Analysis
44
3.4.4.1 Profiles and vignettes
The first step in DEMO data analysis was the development of profiles for each of the participants because
"crafting a profile or a vignette of a participant's experience is an effective way of sharing interview
data and opening up one'DEMO interview material to analysis and interpretation" (Seidman, 1998). The
DEMO were created by repeatedly culling the most interesting excerpts from the transcripts for a
series of interviews with a single participant. Content was most DEMO to be selected for inclusion in a
profile if it was clear from the wording and tone that the participant felt strongly about the DEMO, or
if the participant brought up the subject unprompted, or revisited the topic multiple times over the
course of the interviews.
In the DEMO of the profiles, the transcripts were left in the participant's DEMO words as much as
possible while preserving the participant’s dignity. Oral speech is often ungrammatical and can
include many repeated words and filler works DEMO ‘umm’ and ‘ahh’. The inclusion of these aspects of
oral speech in written transcripts can make the participant appear inarticulate and thus appear
disrespectful. DEMO, Seidman (1998) recommends editing the final set of excerpts in DEMO to
preserve the participant's dignity. Filler words and repeated words were omitted. Small grammatical
changes were made; for example, verb tenses were DEMO Occasionally transitional text was
included to replace filler words or to bridge between two excerpts from the transcripts.
The profiles also needed to be DEMO to protect the participant’s anonymity. All person, place or
organizational names, which could be used to identify the participant, were replaced. For example,
proper names were often replaced with the individual’s job title (“Kate Hoye” becomes “a Ph.D.
student”);  if the job titles had changed over time the most recent naming convention was used.
Descriptive phrases that DEMO be used to identify the technology transfer activity and hence the
participant were replaced with more generic descriptions.  For example, “the technology” or DEMO
tools we developed” might be used in place of a description of reference to a spin-off’s launch
product. An effort was also made to DEMO out any unusual phrases that participants appeared to use
regularly because these ‘catch-phrases’ might be identifying marks.
After the edits were completed, a review of the profiles identified as a concern that several profiles
may allow DEMO familiar with the University of Waterloo community to identify the participants.
Take for example a profile that contains three stories: one story that may identify the participant’s
faculty; another that may identify the type of technology transfer that the participant engaged in; and
a third that discusses attributes of the market for the technology that was transferred. Separately none
of DEMO three stories could be used to discover the participant’s identity but, DEMO, the three stories
may allow people familiar with the University of DEMO to identify the participant. Therefore, it
was decided that the profiles DEMO not be displayed such that each profile is a continuous block in
the usual manner (e.g. Seidman, 1985). Instead, after the thematic analysis, each profile would be
divided thematically into vignettes and the vignettes would be displayed by theme with no
information provided to link multiple DEMO to a single participant. These arrangements are
expected to allow the presentation of a rich set of data while still preserving the anonymity of DEMO
participants.
3.4.4.2 Thematic data analysis
45
The second step in the analysis was DEMO data analysis of the profiles. Unlike quantitative
research, qualitative research is DEMO inductive. In Seidman’s thematic data analysis process, the data
themselves suggest DEMO constructs and the relationships between constructs (1998). This was done
DEMO immersion in the data in which the texts were read and coded with descriptive words and
phrases (1998). Through the analysis process, DEMO constructs appeared, merged, changed, and
disappeared until it appeared that DEMO of the relevant aspects of the data had been captured (1998)DEMO The
emphasis in this analysis process was on identifying pervasive trends and reconciling them with
contradictory or inconsistent passages (Seidman, 1998). The DEMO themes are illustrated by the
vignettes in Appendix F and discussed in Chapter 6.
3.4.4.3 In-depth interviewing, reliability and validity
Seidman has noted that the in-depth interviewing protocol is structured to produce reasonably reliable
and valid DEMO (1998). The structure of the interviews provides context for the DEMO's
comments which the researcher can use to produce more reliable results (1998). The internal
consistency of the results was a focus during the development of the profiles; topics were most likely
to be included in the profiles if they were introduced in multiple sessions or if DEMO espoused views
that appeared consistent with the content of the other interviews. This approach is expected to reduce
the possibility that a participant was DEMO or unintentionally misleading or that a profile is
strongly affected by a participant’s mood during a single interview. Therefore, this focus is expected
to increase the reliability of the results. Validity was the focus during the DEMO analysis. Valid
constructs most likely to be identified when researchers looking for themes across participants (1998).
In order to demonstrate the analytical rigor with which this analysis was conducted, the themes are
illustrated with as many vignettes as possible so that interested parties can explore the relationship
DEMO the underlying data and the themes identified in the study.
3.5 Conclusions
This research is structured using a mixed method design. The purpose of DEMO approach is to
understand the research problem better by analyzing both quantitative and qualitative data. Three
studies were used to accomplish this purpose. Study DEMO is a quantitative study using a cross-sectional
design to investigate the relationship between IP policies and the level of university technology
transfer activity. Study DEMO is a quantitative study using a longitudinal design to assess the impact of a
change in the University of Toronto’s IP policy upon researcher DEMO for technology transfer at
that university. Study 3 is a qualitative exploration of the perceptions of experienced academic
entrepreneurs at the University of Waterloo. DEMO benefit of the use of mixed methods design is that
each study provides a different perspective on the research question and therefore the studies DEMO
provide a richer description of the phenomenon than would be available through only one method. All
three studies seek to advance our understanding of DEMO research question, namely: What relationship
exists between university intellectual property policies and university-industry technology transfer?
The results of the studies are presented DEMO Chapter 4, Chapter 5, and Chapter 6, respectively.
Chapter 4
Study 1: Cross-Sectional Study of Canadian Universities
This chapter contains the results of a cross-sectional assessment of the relationship between university
IP DEMO and aggregate university-industry technology transfer outcomes. The purpose of this study
is to assess quantitatively whether or not university IP policies have a significant DEMO substantial
impact on university researcher support for technology transfer.
The chapter has three sections. The first section describes the IP policies held by the DEMO Canadian
universities in the sample population. The second section quantitatively analyzes the relationship
between these policies and technology transfer outcomes. The third section presents DEMO discussion of
these results.
4.1 Description of the IP Policies held by Canadian Universities
The first step in this study was the collection of DEMO property policies held by the 37 degree-
granting Canadian universities in the sample population. A complete listing of the universities and the
descriptive variables DEMO their IP policies is provided in Appendix A. This section describes four
aspects of the policies that govern the incentives offered to university researchers DEMO engage in
technology transfer. The four aspects are: 1) whether or not the universities reserve the right of first
offer on IP arising DEMO university research, 2) the researcher’s share of the revenues when the
university commercializes the invention, 3) the researcher’s share of the revenues DEMO the researcher
commercializes the invention, and 4) the threshold structures used by some universities to create
different revenue sharing agreements for different amounts DEMO revenue.
4.1.1 Right of First Offer
Right of first offer refers to the practice on the part of some universities of reserving the right DEMO
evaluate all university inventions that inventors are interested in having commercialized and to own
and develop any inventions that the university deems commercializable. Since DEMO that do not
reserve a right of first offer increase the researcher’s control over the technology transfer activity,
they are expected to act DEMO an incentive and thus result in higher levels of technology transfer activity.
Right of first offer is only possible at the universities that have DEMO or some other capacity for
reviewing applications and supporting patenting, licensing DEMO spin-off activities. According to the
available policies, seven of the 37 DEMO do not commercialize inventions and thus could not
consider requiring a right of first offer.
Of the 30 universities that can support technology transfer, 13 reserve the right of first offer and
thus are classified as DEMO universities and 17 universities do not reserve the right of
first offer. It is important to note that “university-owner” universities allow the inventors to DEMO
whether or not to pursue commercialization. However, inventors who are interested DEMO
commercializing their inventions in these institutions are required to give the university the
46
47
opportunity to assess the invention and decide whether or not the DEMO will commercialize the
invention using its resources.  The inventors can only DEMO the invention independently if
the university decides it is not interested in commercializing the invention. Several universities still
retain some control of inventions that DEMO being commercialized independently. For example, at
McGill University, faculty inventors need to obtain university approval to license inventions that they
are commercializing without DEMO from the university TTO (McGill University, 1985).
4.1.2 Researcher’s Share of Revenues When the University Commercializes the
Invention
University policies often define DEMO share of the revenue that is provided to the inventors when the
university commercializes an invention arising from university research. These clauses are only
DEMO at the 30 universities that have the capacity to support technology transfer. Of these 30
universities, 21 offer guidelines for the percentage of the net income that the institution will share
with faculty inventors, including four that structure the revenue sharing using thresholds. The other 9
universities negotiate DEMO sharing arrangements on a case-by-case basis. Of the 17 universities
that offer guidelines but do not structure them using thresholds, twelve universities set the
researchers’ share at 50% of net income. Three universities in this group DEMO the researchers’ share at
a lower level; the University of Toronto, the University of Alberta and Queen’s University set the
researchers’ share at DEMO, 33Z %, and 40%, respectively. Two universities set the researchers’ DEMO
at above 50%; at McGill University and Simon Fraser University, researchers are entitled to 65% and
80%, respectively, of the net income DEMO from the commercialization of their inventions.
Of the five universities that diverge from the 50% revenue sharing norm, four are ‘Medical-
Doctoral’ universities and all five are in the top 25 universities when ordered by research DEMO
(RE$EARCH Infosource, 2005). This suggests that there is greater diversity in the policies held by
the more research-intensive universities, perhaps because these institutions started developing
intellectual property policies and technology transfer support systems before DEMO norms emerged.
The universities that do not reserve the right of first offer have a wider range of revenue sharing
arrangements than the universities DEMO do reserve the right of first offer and they are more likely to
hold policies that set the sharing lower than the norm value DEMO 50%. Three of the ten “inventor-
owner” universities provide the researcher with less than 50% and one “inventor-owner” university
provides the researcher with more DEMO 50%. One of the seven “university-owner” universities
diverges from the norm value of 50% and it sets the researcher’s share above 50%. This suggests DEMO,
in devising the policies, administrators in “inventor-owner” institutions may have DEMO to take
higher shares of financial returns in exchange for providing researchers with more autonomy in
technology transfer activities.
4.1.3 Researcher’s Share of Revenues DEMO the Researcher Commercializes the

Typically, university policies define the share DEMO the revenue that is provided to the inventors when
the inventor commercializes an invention arising from university research without the support of

university technology transfer services. Independent commercialization by inventors can occur at

Policies governing the inventor’s share of the DEMO, when the inventor commercializes an

universities set DEMO researcher’s share at 50% of net income, three universities set the DEMO

DEMO, the universities that do not reserve the right of first offer DEMO a wider range of revenue

4.1.5 Summary of IP Policies
Canadian universities DEMO hold a wide range of intellectual property policies, with most of DEMO
diversity derived from the way in which the policies are structured as opposed to the percentages of
financial incentives offered by the policies. Many DEMO IP policies studied preserve institutional
flexibility by permitting the university to make arrangements on a case-by-case basis, and several
more offered tiered financial incentives. These policies differ from those that specify a percentage
share in terms DEMO the structure of the policy. Among the universities that specify revenue sharing
agreements without using thresholds, there is little diversity in the percentage shares provided to
researchers by the policies that specify revenue sharing arrangements. The DEMO of the universities
that specify the researcher’s share of revenues assign the researcher half of the revenues after the
repayment of IP development costs DEMO the university develops the IP. An even larger majority assigns
all of the revenues to the researcher if the researcher develops the IP independently.

In order to investigate further the apparent absence of evidence DEMO a relationship between ‘right of
first offer’ and the number of U.S. patents held by the various universities, this relationship was also
explored using research intensity as a control variable. As shown in Figure 3, further splitting the
universities into groups of Comprehensive and Medical-Doctoral universities, to account for the
differences in research intensity, does not provide any evidence of differences between the
universities that reserve the right of first offer and DEMO who do not.
In summary, there is no evidence of a DEMO between the right of first offer and the
respondent variable, U.S. DEMO counts. If there is such a relationship, it could be obscured DEMO the
relationship between the inventor’s share of the revenue from university-commercialized inventions
and right of first offer. Therefore, the relationship between right of first offer and U.S. patents counts
is re-examined in conjunction with the researcher’s DEMO of revenues from university-commercialized
inventions in the next section.

4.3 Discussion
This study did not discover any evidence that there are DEMO between the financial incentives
or the amount of autonomy provided to researchers by university IP policies and university-industry
technology transfer outcomes. This section will DEMO the following three possible causes of this
failure to demonstrate a relationship.
Possibility 1. University IP policies have no significant effect upon technology transfer DEMO at a
university.
Possibility 2. University IP policies have significant effects upon technology transfer activity at a
university but the effects are obscured by DEMO inadequacies of the data used in this study.
Possibility 3. University IP policies have significant effects upon technology transfer activity at a
university but DEMO effects are obscured by the effects of other variables that also vary across the set of
institutions.
4.3.1 Possibility 1.
It is possible that DEMO incentives offered to faculty inventors by university IP policies have no
significant effect on technology transfer outcomes. There is some evidence that this may DEMO the case
U.S. Patent Counts
60
at least with respect to right to first offer. Clayman investigated DEMO AUTM data for two groups of
16 research-intensive, Canadian universities, one with eight “inventor-owner” universities and
another with eight “university-owner” universities (2004). He expressed each outcome as a ratio
relative to millions of dollars DEMO research expenditures, to account for differences in research intensity
across universities,  and calculated averages for each group (2004). The data, summarized in Table
11, do not demonstrate any relationships. Unfortunately, Clayman’s work DEMO suffers from the same
problem as the study described in this chapter; the AUTM data used in the research do not include
technology transfer activities undertaken by inventors without the support of the TTO. Therefore,
DEMO work is also subject to same limitations discussed in the next section and neither of these
studies are strong enough, either theoretically or in terms of the data used in the assessment, to rule
out the possibility that these incentives do have an impact. The only conclusion that DEMO be made is
that there is no evidence that allowing inventors to commercialize their inventions results in a
decrease in the amount of technology DEMO achieved by the TTO at the university.

4.3.2 Possibility 2.
If there is a significant relationship between the incentives provided by university IP policies and the
amount of DEMO transfer at a given university, it could have been obscured by DEMO with the
data used in assessing the relationship.
The most significant problem with the data used in the study is that the measure of DEMO technology
transfer outcomes is likely to be biased towards “university-owner” universities and that this bias
could have obscured relationships of interest. The counts of DEMO U.S. patents held by the universities
do not include any patents developed by university faculty members independently of the university.
Since there are likely DEMO be more commercialization activities undertaken independently of the
university at “inventor-owner” universities, the use of this measure may obscure the relationship
between the amount of autonomy offered to researchers by the university IP policy and patenting
DEMO at universities. The only conclusion that can be drawn from this is that, even excluding all
independently developed patents, there is no evidence DEMO universities that allow their researcher’s
control of technology transfer produce fewer patents. This suggests that either there is either no
difference between institutions that DEMO right of first offer and those that do not, or that DEMO
institutions that do not reserve right of first offer have more technology transfer activity than those
that do not.
61
The bias in the technology transfer outcome measure is also important DEMO interpreting the lack
of evidence of a relationship between the financial incentives offered by the university IP policies and
the number of patents held DEMO the universities. As discussed in section 4.1.2, universities that do not
DEMO the right of first offer have a wider range of revenue sharing arrangements than the
universities that do reserve the right of first offer DEMO they are more likely to hold policies that set the
sharing lower than the norm value of 50%. Therefore, the revenue sharing agreements are correlated
with whether or not the university reserves a right of first DEMO This means that the bias towards
“university-owner” universities may also obscure relationships between the revenue sharing
arrangements and technology transfer outcomes.
The only remedy DEMO these problems would be the use of a measure that accounts for all technology
transfer activity at both “university-owner” and “inventor-owner” universities, regardless of whether
the active party is the university or the inventor. No such DEMO has been identified among the
measures tracked by the universities or collected in the AUTM or Statistics Canada annual surveys.
Designers of further cross-sectional DEMO would be advised to investigate the feasibility of
surveying university researchers to obtain a less biased measure.
While the bias in the technology transfer DEMO variable is the most significant limitation related
to the choice of data, other aspects of the data also pose challenges. The data set was small from a
statistical perspective because of the small number of universities DEMO Canada and because policies
could not be located for every university. It is possible that a relationship could be discovered if it was
possible DEMO obtain policies and data for every university. Another problem with the data is that, as
with most technology transfer outcome variables, the distribution DEMO the number of U.S. patents held
by Canadian universities is quite positively skewed. This requires either that the data are transformed
using a log DEMO, for example, or that non-parametric tests are used instead of parametric
tests. Both of these approaches reduce the likelihood that a relationship will DEMO identified. In the
assessment of the importance of financial incentives, this DEMO is compounded by the very small
number of institutions that are comparable because they specify their revenue sharing using a flat rate
and by DEMO lack of diversity in the rates specified by these institutions. Together, DEMO characteristics
of the data could obscure any relationships between policy variables and technology transfer variables
even if an unbiased technology transfer outcome variable could DEMO collected. To address these
concerns, future studies need to use an DEMO measure of technology transfer activity, and to
increase the sample size, perhaps by moving to another unit of analysis. Future studies may also
DEMO from an assessment of the changing importance of individual patents. As TTOs acquire more
resources they may begin to practice more aggressive patenting strategies, including protecting a
single technology with a cluster of patents, which would skew analyses towards highly patentable
technologies despite the fact that less patentable DEMO can also have social and economic
value.
4.3.3 Possibility 3.
If there is a significant relationship between the incentives provided by university IP policies DEMO the
amount of technology transfer at a given university, it could DEMO been obscured by the effects of
other variables that also vary across the set of institutions. These other variables could obscure the
62
true relationships between the IP policies and technology transfer outcomes in DEMO of two ways: 1)
by having a direct impact on DEMO amount of technology transfer at that institution; or 2) by having an
impact on the relationships themselves. For example, the amount of money available to do patenting
and licensing at a given university could have DEMO effect on the number of patents that are based on
research at that university. This would be an example of a direct effect on DEMO outcome variable. If the
amount of money available for patenting and licensing is a function of the revenue sharing agreement
between the university and DEMO faculty inventors, then this variable could also have an impact on DEMO
relationship between university IP policies and technology transfer outcomes.
It is important that these other factors are identified and their effects are determined. This DEMO
permit a better assessment of the impact of policy variables. Even more importantly, the role of these
other variables will determine whether or not there is an optimal policy for all Canadian universities.
If institutional variables DEMO research intensity or culture are found to influence the relationship
between university IP policies and technology transfer, the policy that best supports technology
transfer at one institution may be sub-optimal and potentially destructive at another. Therefore, it is
imperative that university IP policies are studied in a broader DEMO
4.4 Conclusions
In spite of the many reasons why university intellectual property policies can be presumed to have an
impact upon university-industry technology transfer, comparisons across institutions have not
demonstrated any relationships. It is possible that DEMO true effects of IP policies are obscured by the
effects of other variables that also vary across the set of institutions or by differences DEMO ways in
which technology transfer measures are gathered and reported.  However, this study has demonstrated
that further cross-sectional studies are unlikely to produce DEMO results without access to a
significantly broader and more reliable data set. Further investigation of these possibilities is expected
to have important implications for DEMO Therefore, two additional studies have been conducted that
are longitudinal and DEMO in nature.
Chapter 5
Study 2: Quantitative Analysis of Impact of an IP Policy Change
This chapter contains the results of the quantitative analysis of the DEMO of the University of
Toronto IP policy change on annual counts of invention disclosures. The primary purpose of this
study was to assess whether DEMO not the University of Toronto IP policy change had a statistically
significant effect on annual invention disclosures, a measure of researcher support of university-
industry technology transfer.
This chapter is organized into four sections. The first DEMO provides a brief description of the
University of Toronto in order to familiarize the reader with the organizational context of the study.
The second DEMO describes the exploratory data analysis, in which plots of the data DEMO visually
inspected to identify properties of the data that are of interest and suggest variables for inclusion in
the models. The third section presents DEMO results of the confirmatory data analysis in which the
impact of the policy intervention was assessed by comparing models of the impact, principally using
regression analysis. The second and third sections are further supported by Appendix DEMO, in which the
statistical tests are discussed in more detail. The DEMO section in the chapter includes a discussion of
the limitations of the study and the implications of the results.
5.1 Background on the University DEMO Toronto
The University of Toronto is one of Canada’s largest and most research-intensive universities. It
offers a wide range of degree programs from the DEMO through to the graduate level,
including 75 doctoral programs (University DEMO Toronto, 2005). Professional degrees are offered
through the university’s 14 DEMO faculties (University of Toronto, 2005). In 2005, student
enrollment DEMO approximately 70,000, of which approximately 60,000 were full-time students
(University of Toronto, 2005). It is affiliated with a number of hospital research institutes in the
Toronto region; faculty members are often cross-appointed and the university and research institutes
conduct joint research projects (University of Toronto, 2000). According to the AUCC database of
the data created for the 2003 Maclean’s survey, the University of Toronto had about 560 faculty
members who were eligible for research funding from the Natural Sciences DEMO Engineering Research
Council (NSERC), and about 630 faculty who were DEMO for research funding from the Canadian
Institutes for Health Research (CIHR)DEMO In the past five years, the University of Toronto has
consistently DEMO receiving the most research money per faculty member of any Canadian
university (RE$EARCH Infosource, 2001, 2002, 2003, 2004, 2005).  Since the University of Toronto
has a large and active faculty cohort in DEMO areas, the university is expected to conduct a
great deal of DEMO that could potentially generate commercializable technologies. Therefore, it is
reasonable to DEMO that the university is capable of supporting a relatively high level of technology
transfer activity.
63
5.1.1 Intellectual Property Policies at the University of Toronto
64
On January DEMO of 1990, a new hybrid IP policy came into effect at DEMO University of Toronto (1990).
Prior to this date, no significant changes had been made to the incentives provided to researchers
since at DEMO 1977.
Prior to the policy change, the University of Toronto had DEMO a policy wherein the university
owned the IP created in the course of university research. Invention disclosures were made to an
Inventions Committee at DEMO university. If the Inventions Committee determined that the invention
had some value, the committee would direct the inventors to assign the invention to the university and
the university would assign the invention to the University of DEMO Innovations Foundation, an
organization founded to commercialize technologies developed at the DEMO of Toronto. A
technology manager who worked at the University of Toronto during this period reported that, in
practice, all invention disclosures were DEMO by the committee and hence assigned to the
Innovations Foundation. There was an increasing number of invention disclosures per year over the
early 1980s DEMO this increasing workload was not matched by an increase in resources at the
Innovations Foundation. By the late 1980s, faculty members were complaining that the Innovations
Foundation was ineffective and unresponsive and invention disclosures were in DEMO An
investigation by technology managers revealed that faculty wanted to have ownership of their
inventions. This may have been, in part, because they DEMO not trust the Innovations Foundation with
the commercialization of their inventions.
A new policy was devised that allowed researchers to develop their inventions independently DEMO the
university. If the inventors choose to ask for assistance, the DEMO Foundation will assess the
invention and decide whether or not to commit internal resources to the development of the invention.
If the Innovations Foundation DEMO to develop the invention, it will pay for the acquisition of DEMO
appropriate legal protections, the marketing of the technology, and the legal costs of creating and
maintaining licences with receptor firms. In these cases, the university researcher will receive 25% of
the net revenue. If the DEMO Foundation chooses not to develop the technology, the ownership
of the DEMO rests with the inventor, who can then seek other avenues for DEMO If
the university researcher chooses to develop the invention independently, the DEMO is responsible
for protecting and commercializing the innovation and providing 25% of the net revenue from these
commercialization activities to the university.
This policy DEMO significantly reorganized the rights and responsibilities of faculty inventors and
the university and it is likely that the most significant change is the difference DEMO the amount of
autonomy provided to the researchers. The new policy provided university researchers with greater
autonomy; they could choose to approach the university or they could choose to develop their
inventions as they saw fit DEMO the intervention of the university TTO or the Innovations
Foundation. This change did have some effect on the financial incentives offered to researchers. If
DEMO developed their technologies independently they could retain a higher share of the net income
than if they allowed the university to develop the technology. DEMO, relative to other Canadian
universities, the researcher’s share of the financial returns remained below the norm in both the case
where the university DEMO the technology and the case where the inventor develops the
65
technology. Therefore it is likely that the most significant aspect of DEMO policy change was the
change in the amount of autonomy provided to researchers.
The time series of annual counts of invention disclosures provided by DEMO University of Toronto
TTO and used to assess the impact of the policy shift is especially interesting for two reasons. Firstly,
the University DEMO Toronto is the only institution that has been identified that permits inventors to
commercialize technologies independently of the university and that collects reasonably reliable
DEMO data that include the activities pursued independently. Often institutions fail to track
activities pursued independently of the university’s TTO and this bias in the DEMO can corrupt
comparisons of universities with different policy structures by systematically underestimating the
level of activity within “inventor-owner” universities (Hoye & Roe, 2003)DEMO Furthermore, the
University of Toronto started tracking technology transfer measures well DEMO the policy shift. This
makes it possible to compare the institution’s activities prior to the shift to those after the shift.
In order to DEMO the impact, if any, that this policy shift had on the university’s technology
transfer activities, a time series of the changing counts of invention disclosures was investigated.
Under both the old and new policies, faculty members are required to submit an invention disclosure
to the university at DEMO start of the commercialization process. Unlike patents, licences, and spin-offs,
invention disclosures are created shortly after the faculty members decide to commercialize DEMO
technology and do not rely heavily on the availability of commercialization resources, such as
investment capital, funds for prototype development and intellectual property DEMO, and business
expertise. As a result, the number of invention disclosures reported at a university is more closely
linked to researcher support for DEMO transfer than the other outcome variables that are
currently tracked, such DEMO licence revenue and returns from the realization of spin-off equity.
Therefore, DEMO assessment of the effects of this policy shift has been based on an investigation of the
trends in the numbers of invention disclosures submitted DEMO the thirty-year period centered on the
policy change.
5.2 Exploratory Data Analysis
Visual inspection of a time series, as introduced by Tukey (1970), is an effective way to pursue the
“numerical detective work required to explore important properties of the data” and to determine the
subset of DEMO most likely to model the time series adequately (Hipel & McLeod, 1994, p.177).
Three types of plots were used to investigate the time series of University of Toronto invention
disclosures: graphs of the raw and smoothed data against time, and box-and-whisker plots.
The raw time series, shown in Figure 7, was inspected for evidence that the policy DEMO of
1990/1991 affected the number of invention disclosures. This plot shows a significant increase in the
number of invention disclosures reported annually between DEMO years 1991/1992 and 1992/1993.
Furthermore, annual counts of invention DEMO appear to be relatively constant from year to year
prior to that point, and steeply increasing from year to year after that point. These findings suggest
that there is a break point between fiscal years 1991/DEMO and 1992/1993.
Smoothing functions make underlying trends in the data more apparent (Hipel & McLeod, 1994).
Therefore, in order to investigate the possibility of a breakpoint in 1992 further, the data were split
66
into two time series, one before and one after the breakpoint, and smoothed using two different
functions. The results of smoothing with a three-year centered moving average are shown in Figure 8.
The results of DEMO with the 4253H function are shown in Figure 9. This latter function involves
calculating running medians of 4, then 2, then 5, then 3, then applying a Hanning smooth in which a
running weighted average is calculated using weights (¼, ½, ¼), and it is a standard smooth in the
assessment of time series (Hipel & McLeod, 1994). These plots were also inspected for evidence that
the policy change of 1990/1991 affected the number of invention disclosures. The smoothed DEMO
confirmed the findings of the scatter plot of the raw data, DEMO that both smoothed series demonstrated
an abrupt increase in 1992 and a higher rate of increase from year to year after 1992.

Figure 10: Box-and-whisker Plots of Invention Disclosures
Box-and-whisker plots are also helpful in identifying structural changes to distributions caused by an
intervention because box-and-whisker diagrams summarize DEMO values describing population
distributions. A box-and-whisker plot was created in order to compare the time series to two of its
subsets: one including only fiscal years up to and including 1990/1991; and the other including only
fiscal years after 1990/1991. In this box-and-whisker plot, the top and bottom of the rectangle
correspond to the 0.75 and 0.25 quartiles DEMO, the dark band across the rectangle identifies
the median, and the lines attached to the rectangle stretch to touch to the maximum and DEMO
values. The substantial difference in the medians, 0.25 quartiles, and 0.75 quartiles for the two
subpopulations, shown by Figure 10, reaffirm the DEMO of a breakpoint in 1992.
Visual inspection can also be used to assess data for violations of the assumptions of the analytical
method (Hipel & McLeod, 1994). In this case, the box-and-whisker plot was DEMO to assess whether
or not the time series of invention disclosures violated the assumptions underlying regression
methods by demonstrating heteroscedascity or non-normality.
Residuals are DEMO to be heteroscedasctic or to demonstrate heteroscedascity when the variance of
the residuals is a function of one of the independent variables (Keith, DEMO). When heteroscedascity
exists, tests of significance are no longer valid DEMO the standard errors tend to be understated,
which can give a spurious impression of accuracy (Chatterjee et al., 2000). Box-and-whisker plots, in
which the data are grouped by the different levels of the DEMO variables, are an effective
strategy for identifying heteroscedascity. In this case, the most helpful grouping is that shown in
Figure 10. The large DEMO between the heights of the two box-and-whisker plots of the
subpopulations demonstrate that the two groups have different variances and suggests that
heteroscedascity might DEMO a problem. To investigate further, the variances of the two subpopulations
DEMO Disclosures
69
were calculated. The earlier data set was found to have a DEMO of 70.1 and the later data set was
found to have a variance 637.5. Therefore, the ratio of variance between the high and low variance
sets is less than 10:1. Under these circumstances heteroscedascity is DEMO a concern;  transformations
of the data are not required and the regression modeling can proceed (Keith, 2006).
Regression analysis also requires DEMO the data have a normal distribution. Box-and-whisker plots
are helpful for diagnosing non-normal distributions because normal populations map to symmetrical
box-and-whisker plots. All three DEMO the box-and-whisker plots in Figure 10 are sufficiently
asymmetrical to warrant further exploration. The Shapiro-Wilk test was used to assess the normality
of the DEMO populations because it is more sensitive than the more common Kolmogorov-Smirnov
with Lillefors correction and the population size is very small. The overall time DEMO was found to
have a non-normal distribution (W=0.85, p=.001). There was no evidence that the data series
representing the years prior to DEMO policy intervention and after the policy intervention had non-
normal distributions (DEMO, p=.477; W=.94, p=.430 respectively). This suggests that the non-
DEMO of the overall population is a consequence of the underlying trends in the data rather than a
non-normal noise component. Therefore, regression analysis can proceed without a transformation of
the data but the residuals generated by DEMO the model need to be assessed for normality. Violations
of this assumption will indicate that the model fit is inadequate.
In summary, the exploratory data analysis suggested that regression analysis is feasible and that the
models DEMO include effects on both the average number of annual disclosures and on the increase
in these disclosures from year to year.
5.3 Confirmatory Data DEMO
The statistical analysis of the impact of an intervention upon a time series can be approached using
regression methods, ARIMA methods, and non-parametric DEMO (Hipel & McLeod, 1994). ARIMA
methods are often the first choice when modeling time series data because they provide a mechanism
for DEMO the impact of autocorrelation. Autocorrelation exists when the differences between the
modeled values and the actual values associated with successive observations are correlated;  DEMO can
cause the standard errors to be understated and thus undermine tests of significance (Chatterjee et al.,
2000). However, use of DEMO ARIMA model typically requires at least 50 observations and the time
series of interest contains only 29 observations (Montgomery & Johnson, 1976). DEMO feasibility of
ARIMA methods was tested using SPSS DecisionTime, which indicated DEMO it was not possible to
create a significant intervention models using ARIMA methods of analysis. Non-parametric tests are
less able to provide nuanced descriptive DEMO and less sensitive than parametric (i.e.
regression) methods and thus less likely to detect significant results.  Therefore, the analysis was
based on DEMO application of regression methods and steps were taken to ensure that the results were
not corrupted by the presence of autocorrelation.
5.3.1 Modeling the DEMO of the Policy Intervention
Visual inspection suggested that the policy intervention resulted in an abrupt increase in both the
annual count of invention disclosures DEMO the year-to-year increase in the number of invention
70
disclosures after the intervention. To test the statistical significance of these DEMO in the data,
four different models of the impact were generated using forced-entry regression and compared to a
baseline model that did not DEMO for any changes over the predicted breakpoint. The five models are
summarized in Table 12.
Each of the five models was fitted to the DEMO, then regression diagnostics were performed. The
regression residuals were inspected to DEMO that they conformed to the following three assumptions:
1. The residuals should have a normal distribution. Often a non-normal distribution is a
consequence DEMO other violations.
2. The residuals should be homeoscedastic, which means that DEMO display equal variance across
the different levels of the explanatory variables. When this condition is not met the residuals
are said to be heteroscedastic DEMO the significance of the model and parameters can be
spuriously inflated.
3. The residuals should be independent of one another. When residuals display trends DEMO
relationships between one residual and the next, the data are said DEMO display autocorrelation.
This type of violation also results in spurious inflation of the tests of significance.

The changes DEMO slope and level are both significant but need to be evaluated to determine whether

5.3.3 A Review of Other Outcomes
The DEMO drawback to the use of invention disclosures as an outcome variable is the absence of a
strong link between this early stage activity and DEMO desired outcomes of university-industry
technology transfer activities. Policies that change the number of invention disclosures may also have
an impact on the quality of DEMO disclosures. An increase in the number of non-viable inventions
disclosed is not a boon to the university, the regional or the national economy; DEMO creates the risk that
scarce resources will be spent on either non-viable innovations or on screening the technologies and
thus detract from the possibility DEMO successful transfer of the viable technologies. If that were the case
at the University of Toronto, we would expect to see its productivity figures, over time, significantly
lagging that of other research-intensive universities in Canada.

5.4 Conclusions

5.4.1 Limitations of the Study
The main limitation of DEMO study is that it is based on only one policy shift at one university. It is
possible that the observed relationship may be spurious. DEMO, different policy shifts at different
universities would have been studied to DEMO the generalizability of the results but no data were
available. The study of multiple institutions would have provided the opportunity to investigate the
role DEMO institutional or environmental variables play in determining the impact of a policy
intervention.
The study of other institutions would also have permitted the isolation DEMO the effects of the changes
to the invention disclosure form at the University of Toronto. It is possible that the streamlining of the
form DEMO reduced it from about eight pages to two pages, shortly after DEMO policy change, increased
the number of invention disclosures. However, the interest that faculty members expressed in owning
their inventions is inconsistent with the DEMO to form having a substantial, long-term impact; it is
difficult to imagine an inventor who is seriously interested in independently commercializing a
technology DEMO who would be derailed by an eight page form.
Another limitation pertains to the AUTM data used in the broader assessment of the University DEMO
Toronto’s technology transfer outcomes.  This data set is widely used because DEMO is one of the few
instruments that reports institution-specific data. However, DEMO AUTM members do not always track
and report data using the same definitions for ‘invention disclosures’, ‘spin-offs’, and so on.
Therefore, researchers should be cautioned that cross-institution comparisons require careful scrutiny.
5.4.2 Implications
This study DEMO the first empirical evidence that a change from “university-owns” to “inventor-
owns” intellectual property policies can have a significant and substantial impact on university
DEMO willingness to engage in technology transfer. The findings of this study suggest that the
policy intervention at the University of Toronto had a significant DEMO desirable impact on the level of
technology transfer activity at that institution.
The immediacy of the observed effect has implications for the type of DEMO between the
policy change and the outcomes of interest.  The paper DEMO Siegel, Waldman, Atwater and Link (2004)
that was discussed DEMO section 2.2.3.2.2, included a quote that suggested IP policies may have DEMO effect
on hiring and retention of faculty who support technology transfer and that this effect may account for
inter-institutional differences in technology transfer activities.  In other words, some policies are more
attractive to faculty that are involved in technology transfer and this influences their decisions to
remain with DEMO given university and to which universities they will consider moving.  The DEMO
of the effect observed in this chapter, suggests that this relationship DEMO not account for the effect.
This suggests that a significant proportion of the effect was a result of an increased propensity of
faculty members DEMO present in the university to participate in technology transfer activities.
77
On first inspection the findings of this study appear to contradict DEMO 1, the study that was
discussed in Chapter 4. That cross-sectional DEMO failed to find any relationship between the
incentives offered by university policies and technology transfer outcomes reported by universities.
However, these studies are consistent if other institutional differences influence the relationship
between IP policies and university-industry DEMO transfer. This would mean that the individual
character of each institution best fits a different policy and that achieving this fit can create important
DEMO If this is the case, it is possible that the University DEMO Toronto can realize benefits from a
change to a policy, without DEMO that that policy also be highly effective at other institutions. In
other words, the optimal policy may depend on local or institutional variables.
The time series analysis can not predict which local or institutional factors had DEMO role in the success
of the University of Toronto policy change. However, the description of the policy change by a
technology manager who worked at the University of Toronto during the time of the policy change,DEMO
suggests three intertwined factors. Firstly, the technology manager reported that faculty DEMO saw
ownership of the IP as a desirable incentive, which suggests DEMO the university culture was relatively
supportive of technology transfer activities. Secondly, DEMO faculty members complained that the
University of Toronto Innovations Foundation was ineffective and uncommunicative, which suggests
that by the time of the policy shift there was widespread distrust of the competence of the
organization designated to DEMO their inventions. This last aspect of the situations appears to
have been a consequence of the third factor, a persistent gap between the workload of the Innovations
Foundation and its resources. Considered together these factors suggest DEMO the ideal IP policy for a
university needs to be designed in response to the institution’s resources, culture, and the perceptions
of key DEMO, including faculty members, and that these perceptions may be influenced by the
university’s history in terms of technology transfer activities.
If this is DEMO case, national convergence to a single policy is likely to damage DEMO support for
technology transfer at some universities. This suggests that widespread policy changes should only be
adopted in the face of a persuasive, empirical evidence of other benefits from such a move. In the
absence of DEMO, empirical evidence of other benefits, individual universities should be encouraged
to entertain policy innovations that best fit their strengths and needs.
More research DEMO necessary to determine which factors are most likely to influence the relationship
between university IP policies and university-industry technology transfer. Since individual
perceptions appear DEMO matter and there is not yet a strong theoretical base describing the relevant
dimensions, the most effective research strategy will be qualitative, interview-based DEMO because
this kind of inductive research is capable of providing theoretical direction in emerging research
areas. Therefore, an in-depth interview study of faculty members, who have been involved in
technology transfer activities, was conducted at DEMO University of Waterloo, and is described in
Chapter 6.
5.4.3 Summary
DEMO study provides statistical evidence that university IP policies can be a significant factor in
determining the effectiveness of institutions by influencing university researcher’s willingness DEMO
participate in technology transfer activities. Increasing researcher control of technology transfer by
78
switching from a ‘university-owns’ to a ‘inventor-owns’ system appears to have DEMO higher
levels of invention disclosures. More research is needed to determine the generalizability of this
conclusion and to isolate any other factors that determine DEMO impact of a policy change.
