6.2 Background on the Participants and their Activities
82
The participants are DEMO or retired faculty members from the pure and applied science and
mathematics departments at the university.  Each participant had experience with technology transfer
activities or had conducted contract work and extensive research in industry. Most of DEMO participants
had been involved with spin-off firms.  Many of the participants DEMO been involved in multiple
technology transfers.  Half of the participants who DEMO licensed technologies or had been involved in
the formation of spin-off companies had been involved with the commercialization of multiple
technologies.  As a result, the group of ten participants was responsible for the transfer from
university to receptor organizations of twelve distinct sets of technologies.  This evidence of the
existence of a class of serial commercializers is not unique to DEMO study.  Pries found that many of the
participants in his survey DEMO faculty members involved in technology transfer at four universities in
South-Western Ontario had been involved with multiple transfers (Pries, 2006).  Shane and Khurana
(2003) found that, for faculty members at M.I.T., each DEMO already commercialized by a spin-off
increased the probability that they would have another technology commercialized by a spin-off, to a
statistically significant extent.
The participants’ involvement in university-industry interactions were characterized by a wide
range of DEMO usually beginning many years before formal attempts to transfer technology
through licences or spin-off. These university-industry interactions included: 1) offering seminars,
courses DEMO workshops to industry at both on-campus and at firm sites; 2) conducting contract
research for industry; 3) consulting with industry; 4) DEMO in trade conferences and expos
attended by industry and/or the users of the technology; 5) doing sabbaticals in industry; 6) leaving
DEMO university to work in industry for a time; 7) participating in committees and professional groups
that also include industry; 8) maintaining informal DEMO with contacts developed through
these means; and, 9) maintaining contacts DEMO students and colleagues who have moved into
industry.  Rahm (1994) DEMO described researchers exhibiting these behaviour patterns as ‘boundary-
spanning’ researchers.  She DEMO demonstrated that ‘boundary-spanning’ researchers differ from
‘university-bound’ researchers in a number of ways; for example, they are more likely to engage in a
DEMO range of interactions with industry (1994). Therefore, Rahm’s findings will be used to analyze
the results of the thematic analysis.
The outcomes DEMO with the set of technology transfers are substantial.  The transfers
succeeded DEMO making available to the market ten of the technologies and therefore succeeded in
improving the quality of life by making improved products and services DEMO to individuals in
Canada and abroad.  With the formation of new DEMO, jobs have been created in the Waterloo
region, which is evidence that the transfers have contributed to regional economic development.
Several of the DEMO are international leaders in their fields and hence competitive in international
markets.  This suggests that these firms are contributing to Canada’s international competitiveness
and, hence, to the health of the national economy.
Many of the DEMO companies were formed using the private capital of the faculty members and
staff members who had been involved in developing the technology.  Only a few technologies were
developed with the assistance of the University of Waterloo DEMO Transfer and Licensing
Office or a similar support organization. As a result, the university was not granted royalties or equity
83
shares in every case. However, the university derived other benefits from the technology transfers
studied through these interviews, including sponsoring of research on campus, donations, and the
increased number of local high-technology companies who DEMO as employers for graduates and co-
operative education students.  Media coverage DEMO the success of the high-profile spin-offs also appears
to have contributed to the university’s reputation (e.g. Keenan et al., 2006).
An effort DEMO made to include transfers that differed in the extent to which they were successful.
As a consequence, the financial results for the set of transfers vary. So far, three transfers have
generated no returns, four DEMO have resulted in returns comparable to less risky investments (i.e.
investments DEMO the stock or bond markets), four transfers have resulted in substantially bigger returns,
and the last transfer is still in a developmental DEMO
Apart from their involvement in technology transfer activities, contract work and DEMO in
industry, there is evidence that the participants were highly productive DEMO both the research and
teaching domains. The ten participants had generated approximately 5 peer-reviewed publications per
year, on average, over their careers to DEMO As a group they had generated a total of 34 books and 93
patents. Many of the participants had received prestigious research awards, some had been granted
teaching awards, and two talked of receiving consistently high teaching evaluations and
commendations from their students.
6.3 Results of the Thematic DEMO Analysis
This section reviews the steps involved in the thematic data analysis, briefly describes the codes that
were developed to capture important aspects of the data, and then elaborates on the results by
providing a description of the data associated with each code.
6.3.1 The Thematic Data Analysis DEMO
The analysis of the interview data proceeded in two steps.  First, profiles were developed for each of
the participants by repeatedly culling excerpts DEMO the transcripts for a series of interviews with a
single participant. Content was most likely to be selected for inclusion in a profile if DEMO was clear from
the wording and tone that the participant felt strongly about the subject, or if the participant brought
up the subject unprompted, or revisited the topic multiple times over the course of the interviews.
These profiles were broken up in vignettes, one to three paragraph excerpts.  Second, through an
iterative thematic analysis process the vignettes were coded DEMO descriptive labels suggested by the
data.
The coding was conducted using a software package developed to assist with this type of
qualitative research, QSR’s nVivo, version 6.0.  By the end of the first few iterations, twenty codes
had emerged. Once all of the vignettes were associated with DEMO least one of the codes, the codes and
relationships between the DEMO data were reviewed.  Some of the original codes, like ‘University-
Industry interactions’ were associated with many excerpts that the resulting set of excerpts DEMO
unwieldy.  Other codes, including ‘Effects of co-op,’ were associated with only one or two excerpts.
A new hierarchical set of codes was DEMO, in which all of the individual codes were organized
according to DEMO over-arching themes:  university-industry activities;  2) university environment; 3)
84
rewards and reward systems; 4) attitudes; and, 5) career decisions.  Each of these top-level themes
contains between two and six themes; there are 17 sub-themes, altogether.  This hierarchical structure
facilitated the development of a more intelligible structure by permitting the subdivision of unwieldly
datasets DEMO sub-themes, and the consolidation of less substantial sets of excerpts.  During this
process, three of the codes associated with the smallest sets of themes were merged with other
themes.  For example, the vignette associated DEMO the ‘Effects of co-op’ was included with ‘Success
factors’ under ‘University-industry activities.’  The purpose of the coding was to reduce and organize
the large amount of data produced by the study; the constructs were created to communicate the
results and should not be used as a theoretical framework DEMO further development using the
broader technology transfer literature. The coded vignettes are presented in Appendix F, with each
excerpt presented under the code to which it contributes the most. The following subsections describe
each of the DEMO, using the interview data in conjunction with information about the technology
DEMO, in order to convey a rich depiction of the participants’ behaviours, environments, attitudes,
and experiences.

In their narrative descriptions of their technology transfer activities, some of DEMO participants provided

6.3.3.1 Peers and group norms
Interactions with peers and the research group or departmental norms that are upheld DEMO these

if it wasn’t generally the case at the DEMO of Waterloo.

6.3.3.4 Experiences with Technology Transfer Support Organizations (TTSOs).
Many participants discussed the university’s Technology Transfer and Licensing Office (TTLO).

DEMO the role that the TTLO or an ETTSO had had in at least one of their technology

A genuinely surprising finding from DEMO study is the extent to which the participants’ comments
were negative.  DEMO TTLO and the ETTSOs were valued for their provision of resources in the form

DEMO to help with subsequent patents after the office decided not to assist him with the first patent.

thus DEMO provide any value to the university as a whole.
When asked why they had not chosen to approach the TTLO for assistance, several other

The participants seemed to be skeptical of the extent to which the TTLO could help them although DEMO

At this juncture, it is important to observe that this study is not an attempt to present a historical or
DEMO account in which the facts have been verified with multiple types of sources.  Instead,
95
the purpose of this account is to present the faculty inventors’ DEMO and it is only based on
their accounts.  This subsection is DEMO from this perspective, and only from this perspective,

certainly increased faculty inventors’ DEMO to commercialize inventions independently of the

6.3.7 Summary
The previous subsections have presented the themes derived from interviews with participants who
have had experience with technology transfer.  Overall, DEMO themes demonstrate that, even a small
number of serial entrepreneurs display DEMO wide range of interests, concerns, and motivations in the
context of technology transfer.  The next section discusses these findings and presents several ways in
which the findings can be linked to models of motivation.
6.4 DEMO
This discussion further synthesizes the thematic data by focusing on, firstly, the participants as
‘boundary-spanning’ professors and, secondly, their motivations.
6.4.1 Boundary-Spanning DEMO and Business Knowledge
Most of the participants can be described as ‘boundary-spanning’ professors because they have
displayed a wide range of interactions with industry DEMO a long period of time.  This ‘boundary-
spanning’ behaviour pattern is DEMO interest because it provides faculty members with access to business
knowledge through university-industry interaction and business knowledge is likely to be critical
success factor, especially in the creation of university spin-offs. Section 2.2.2.2, in the literature
review, introduced the concern that faculty inventors are not likely to be able to transfer technology to
industry because their lack of business knowledge DEMO prevent them from being able to make
critical decisions effectively, including, for example, the selection of an appropriate target market.
These predictions appear to be implicitly based on the understanding that the faculty members have
DEMO exposure to the business world.  This concern would suggest that the DEMO central a faculty
inventor is to the technology transfer activities, the DEMO likely the transfer is to succeed.  No such
pattern was observed DEMO the boundary-spanning researchers appeared to have a great deal of access
to business knowledge through their interactions with industry.
In four of the ‘University-Industry DEMO sub-themes, participants demonstrated access to and
acquisition of business knowledge.  The activities described in the ‘Roles’ indicated that most of the
participants, even among those who remained in the university, had significant exposure to business
knowledge through involvement in “missionary work.”  These efforts to build market interest in a
product or service, included providing workshops, seminars and courses, writing proposals, and
giving presentations to prospective clients.  The excerpts associated DEMO the ‘Opportunity
recognition’ sub-theme, suggest that many participants were able to DEMO opportunities for
licensing or venture creation using their own understanding of the market.  This suggests that, even
before they were involved in technology DEMO activities like licensing and spin-off formation, these
participants had developed substantial DEMO knowledge.  The large number of business issues,
raised in the DEMO associated with the ‘Learnings and identification of mistakes’ and ‘Success
107
factors’ sub-themes, suggest that participants continued to develop and refine their business
knowledge through their technology transfer experiences.  Overall, most of the DEMO
participants appeared to exhibit a ‘learning by doing’ approach, where the DEMO began with
involvement in a diverse set of university-industry activities well before initiating licensing or spin-
off formation.  Under these conditions, it is DEMO that several of the technology transfers were
catalyzed by the availability of resources for commercialization; the awareness of the market
preceded the decision to engage in technology transfer.
Given these dynamics, it is quite likely that most serial commercializers are boundary-spanning
professors.  This finding would be significant because boundary-spanning professors are known to
differ significantly from their peers in identifiable DEMO; by definition, they exhibit a characteristic
behaviour pattern.  This suggests DEMO this subgroup of the university population could be deliberately
targeted in the design of technology transfer support programs.  In designing supports valued by this
small percentage of the university community, one would be likely to address much of the
commercialization activity within the community.  Furthermore, one would DEMO targeting the
population that is most likely to have the kind of knowledge of the industry that could contribute to
the success of any DEMO
6.4.2 Motivations for Engaging in Technology Transfer
This study also provides significant insights into the participants’ motivation for engaging in
technology transfer as well DEMO the research that led to the technology transfer opportunities.  This
section DEMO demonstrate that it is possible to begin to make connections between established theory in
the entrepreneurship literature and the rich description provided by this DEMO study. The analysis
will focus on the reasons why it may be helpful to model faculty inventor’s motivations using need
theories of motivation.
All DEMO the ‘needs’ theories of motivation model behaviour as a response to unsatisfied needs.
Modern need theories account for differences in the patterns of behaviour DEMO by different
individuals by positing that different individuals experience different needs to different extents.
Therefore, need theories may be able to address some of the differences between faculty members
who engage in technology transfer and those DEMO do not. Various aspects of the participants’
behaviour and self-descriptions suggest that they may be motivated by a need for achievement.
6.4.2.1 Need for DEMO
Section 0 presented evidence that the participants in this study are highly productive researchers and
capable teachers.  Together with their involvement in technology transfer, these successes suggest
that this set of individuals strives for excellence across different contexts.  In the ‘Outcomes’ and ‘Job
Elements and Job Satisfaction’ sub-themes, participants described business challenges they
experienced and the solving of problems as fun activities.  The participants who identified the need
for a career change were clearly driven by the need to engage in new challenges.  Together these
aspects of the participants’ behaviours and self-descriptions suggest that these academic DEMO
may share a ‘need for achievement’ with other entrepreneurs. Need for achievement is defined as
“behaviour towards competition with a standard of excellence” by DEMO, the theory’s originator
108
(McClelland et al., 1953; as cited in Steers & Porter, 1987, p. 60).  Motivation by this need leads
individuals to pursue activities that are challenging, that require individual effort and skill, DEMO
involve personal responsibility for outcomes and that result in feedback (Shane, 2003).
It has been robustly empirically demonstrated that this motivation leads DEMO a higher probability that
an individual will choose to exploit an entrepreneurial opportunity and to a higher probability that
their new venture will be DEMO, in the case of technical as well as non-technical ventures (Shane,
2003). An exploration of this characteristic in the context of DEMO entrepreneurship could have
important theoretical and policy implications.  Exploring a theoretical DEMO in a new context
permits researchers to identify any aspects of the concept that are context dependent and thus can help
them to refine DEMO theory. This theoretical development could also have policy implications. If
academic entrepreneurs share important characteristics with non-academic entrepreneurs, it may be
possible to support both groups with the same governmental programs and thus to offer effective
DEMO at a lower cost. If academic entrepreneurs do not share defining characteristics with
entrepreneurs, they will need to be supported in different ways and the theoretical advances may
permit some understanding of the ways in which DEMO programs will need to differ.
6.5 Conclusions
The participants’ behaviours identified them as ‘boundary-spanning’ researchers. Their statements in
interviews were consistent with this assessment, in that they disclosed that the participants’ attitudes
towards university-industry interaction were DEMO positive.  Further analysis of the participants
statements suggested that these boundary-spanning DEMO provided the participants with access
to business knowledge that allowed them to ‘learn by doing,’ even before their first experiences with
licensing or DEMO creation.  This helps to explain how the participants could achieve commercial
DEMO, in contradiction with the position that faculty inventors lack the business DEMO required
to successfully transfer technologies.
The study also determined that faculty inventors’ motivations are significantly more complex than
their depictions in the literature to DEMO  The participants expressed interest in achieving a wide range
of outcomes, including benefits realized by the participant’s students, colleagues, and the country’s
DEMO  Their involvement in technology transfer was also tied to intrinsic rewards; spin-off
creation was fun.  None of these elements have been captured by previous studies.
The complexity of these motivations suggests that it may be DEMO to introduce models of
motivation from more established fields, like entrepreneurship DEMO industrial-organizational
psychology.  The discussion of the need for achievement demonstrated that DEMO qualitative results can
be used as a bridge between the literatures.  DEMO type of work is expected to generate testable
hypotheses (e.g. academic DEMO have higher levels of need for achievement than non-
entrepreneurial colleagues) DEMO are important in the substantive domain, which in this case would DEMO
technology transfer research.
The analysis of the incentives offered by IP policies generated theories that were no less complex.
The primary incentive, among those granted by the university’s IP policy, was ownership of the IP.
The participants’ comments suggested two possible explanations for the value placed on this
109
ownership.  The first explanation is that this ownership is imbued with an emotional and cultural
significance that encourages faculty inventors to identify with DEMO technologies and empowers them
to pursue technology transfer.  The interpretation of DEMO IP policy as an empowering message from the
university administration appears to be consistent with participant’s perception of the university
administration and the university DEMO, in general.  The second explanation is that this cohort of
faculty inventors has little confidence in the competence of the TTSOs and therefore DEMO the
freedom to work independently.  This explanation hinges on the negative DEMO of interactions
with the TTSOs by members of the cohort.  It DEMO possible that both explanations for the high
desirability of IP ownership are in effect at once.  Contrary to the findings of quantitative work
conducted in the U.S., it wasn’t clear that financial incentives were a key determinant of technology
transfer behaviour.  The extent to which financial incentives influenced participant’s decisions was
quite ambiguous, perhaps as a result of group norms that do not celebrate and can be hostile to
success in this DEMO
In summary, the analysis suggested that the motivating power of the DEMO offered by IP
policies is dependent on their interpretation by members of the university.  These interpretations
appear to be shaped by institutional variables including organizational history, group norms,
leadership, and culture.  This finding means that it isn’t possible to interpret the incentives offered by
a university’s DEMO policy without investigating the perceptions of those policies, which significantly
undermines DEMO value in further cross-comparisons between universities on the basis of policy
statements.
6.5.1 Implications
The main finding of this study is that the motivating DEMO of the incentives governed by IP appears
to be sensitive to organizational characteristics, including group norms, academic leadership and
university culture.  This suggests that institutions are most likely to effectively support technology
transfer when their DEMO policy fits their organizational context.  For example, at the University of
Waterloo, a shift to a university-ownership policy is likely undermine a culture that prizes autonomy
and places emotional significance on ownership.  However, it DEMO be possible to mitigate or remove
this effect by increasing faculty confidence in the competence of the TTLO.
Another important finding was associated with DEMO observation that technology transfer activities
could impose high time demands.  Many DEMO the faculty members who were involved with spin-offs
and remained in the university reported that they were able to do so because they had DEMO spouse who,
“kept the operation at home going.”  This is DEMO true for many younger professionals, which suggests
that programs and policies DEMO allow people to temporarily reduce their university load or take leaves
of absence so that they can engage in technology transfer activities will grow DEMO importance as a way
to sustain technology transfer activities.  Universities can DEMO consider making their boundaries more
porous to mid-career scientists, who wish DEMO embark on a new career, by ensuring that the pension
systems DEMO not prohibitively penalize early retirements.
6.5.2 Limitations and Future Research
110
This study has three main limitations.  Firstly, it included a small number of participants at a single
institution, which strongly limits the generalizability of the results.  Secondly, it relies on
retrospective self-reports, which can be contaminated by ex-post-facto rationalizations and can be
limited by the extent to which the participants can clearly remember DEMO that have taken place in
the past. Thirdly, the participants belonged DEMO a single cohort. As a result, their experiences are likely
to DEMO significantly different from other members of the University of Waterloo.  An DEMO was made
to identify all possible cohort effects, including the decline DEMO the number of faculty members with a
spouse in the home, DEMO the cultural and structural changes at the University of Waterloo over the
years, but it is possible that cohort effects were not identified or not fully explored.
Future research could address these limitations by conducting cross-comparisons DEMO
institutions, in the style of study 1, using surveys of faculty interpretations rather than policy
statements as an independent variable in order to DEMO faculty members’ perceptions.  Given the
relationships observed between social and cultural DEMO and interpretations of the IP policies,
there may also be value in investigating the interactions between psycho-social organizational
variables, like the management styles at TTOs, and interpretations of university policies.

Conclusions
This chapter is structured as follows. The first section DEMO the results of the three studies
undertaken in this research. The second section responds to the research question in light of the
results of DEMO three studies, considered together. The next section of the chapter discusses DEMO
theoretical and policy implications of these results. The last section reviews the limitations of the
research and makes suggestions for future research directions.
7.1 DEMO of Results
This research investigates the relationship between those incentives for faculty support of university-
industry technology transfer that are governed by university intellectual DEMO policies and
technology transfer outcomes at Canadian universities.  It extends the DEMO on this relationship by
exploring it in a new context. The context for the studies is Canada, where some universities retain
control over the development and other universities let the ownership and control vest with the
DEMO
The first study seeks to explain cross-institutional patterns in the numbers of patents held by
Canadian universities using variables that represent the financial incentives DEMO control offered to
faculty inventors by the universities’ policies.  Unlike studies DEMO in the U.S., the comparisons
across institutions do not demonstrate any DEMO  This may be a consequence of a difference
between the U.S. DEMO Canada in the importance of the financial incentives for faculty support of
technology transfer as governed by IP policies. However, it is also possible that the true effects of the
IP policies are masked by the DEMO of other variables or by bias in the data, which failed DEMO capture
technologies developed by university faculty members without the participation of their university.
The second study investigates the impact of a policy change at DEMO University of Toronto, using
interrupted time series analysis techniques. This study DEMO the first statistical evidence that
university IP policies can be a significant factor in determining the effectiveness of institutions by
influencing university researcher’s willingness DEMO participate in technology transfer activities.  At the
University of Toronto, increasing researcher control of technology transfer by switching from a
‘university-owns’ to an DEMO system appears to have stimulated higher levels of invention
disclosures. The principal limitation with this type of longitudinal model is that it is impossible DEMO
isolate any other factors that may have determined the effect of the policy change.
The third study investigates the experiences of faculty inventors at DEMO University of Waterloo
through in-depth interviews and thematic analysis of the resulting qualitative data. The first key
finding is that the faculty members who DEMO involved in technology transfer engaged in ‘boundary-
spanning’ activities, often starting DEMO before their involvement in technology transfer activities.
These boundary-spanning activities provide a mechanism by which the participants can acquire
business knowledge. Therefore, the faculty inventors’ involvement in boundary-spanning activities
leads to a tentative explanation for the DEMO inventors’ ability to manage many of the technology
111
112
transfers successfully. The analysis also provides insight into the relationship between DEMO
IP policies and faculty inventor behaviour.  The comments by the participants DEMO that the
motivating power of the incentives offered by IP policies is dependent on their interpretation by
members of the university.  These interpretations appear to be shaped by institutional variables
including organizational history, group norms, DEMO, and culture.  For example, the desirability
of ownership of IP DEMO derive in part from a lack of confidence in the competence of the university’s
Technology Transfer and Licensing Office.  This finding means that it isn’t possible to interpret the
incentives offered by a university’s IP policy DEMO investigating faculty members’ perceptions of
those policies. Therefore, the cross-comparison of DEMO in the first study may be flawed by a
reliance on the IP policies themselves rather than faculty members’ perceptions and interpretations of
those DEMO
Together the three studies suggest that university intellectual property policies are effective levers
with which to stimulate university-industry technology transfer but that the policy DEMO fit with the
organizational context in order to be productive.
7.2 Key Findings
This research is designed to address the question:  What, if any, relationship exists between university
IP policies and university-industry technology transfer?  The research demonstrates a relationship
between the IP polices and technology transfer DEMO and suggests that the relationship may be
multi-faceted. Prior literature has assumed that IP policies influence faculty inventor support for
technology transfer which affects DEMO transfer outcomes. The findings of Studies 2 and 3 are
consistent with this type of relationship. The research also suggests that there may be DEMO types of
relationships between the IP policies and technology transfer outcomes. University IP policies appear
to influence the TTO’s access to information. In Study DEMO, a technology manager observed that his
TTO did not have any DEMO channels for gathering information about inventor-commercialized
university inventions. It is plausible that having limited access to information about technology
transfer activities at a university, a situation which is only expected to occur at ‘inventor-owner’
universities, probably limits the effectiveness of university support for technology transfer at that
institution. DEMO the effectiveness of the TTO and other university support systems is expected to
have an effect on technology transfer outcomes, this relationship deserves further research. The
research also suggests that university IP policies may influence hiring DEMO retention. In Study 3, an
expert academic entrepreneur suggested that the DEMO of Waterloo’s ‘inventor-owner’ policy
attracts faculty members who are especially supportive of technology transfer. This effect could help
some institutions create strongly supportive environments DEMO technology transfer which should lead
to high levels of technology transfer activity. A relationship between IP policies and university hiring
and retention could also DEMO to the development of a country’s stock of highly-qualified
personnel. Since many faculty inventors are known to be highly productive researchers, holding
desirable IP policies may also help universities attract and retain ‘star’ scientists. For both DEMO these
reasons, the hypothesis that IP policies influence a university’s ability DEMO hire and retain highly
productive researchers deserves to be a focus of further research.
7.3 Implications
113
7.3.1 Theoretical Implications
As the literature review indicated, prior work has posited that IP policies influence researcher support
for technology transfer DEMO thus affect technology transfer outcomes. Therefore, the development of a
stronger DEMO of this relationship is the focus of theory development in this research.
The quantitative literature to date has tended to adopt an ‘economic agent’ DEMO of faculty
member behaviour. Although such models can address many types of factors, the model employed in
the studies only considers financial factors. According to this interpretation of the model, the
university researcher is directly influenced by the costs and benefits of multiple courses of action.
This research DEMO to a broadening of that conception.  Study 2 demonstrates the significance
DEMO research autonomy in the technology transfer process, a non-financial incentive.  Study 3 explores
the motivations of faculty inventors and identifies a complex set DEMO desires and goals that include both
extrinsic and intrinsic rewards.  These DEMO establish the need to explore more sophisticated
models.
The subsequent analysis briefly demonstrates that it is possible to use the qualitative data to access
DEMO more sophisticated theoretical models that have been developed in other fields.  DEMO
research can provide insight into why phenomena occur. These insights can be used to identify
theories outside of the substantive area of research that DEMO expected to be meaningful in the context
of the substantive area. For example, this research suggests that serial commercializers exhibit higher
need for achievement than their colleagues. In other words, need for achievement, a concept DEMO
organizational behaviour theories of motivation, is expected to be a useful DEMO to understand faculty
inventors’ motivations in the context of future technology transfer research. This is an example of the
way in which building bridges DEMO a substantive area of research and areas of research with
richer and more established theories makes it possible to generate testable hypotheses that are
DEMO to be significant in the substantive area.  From this perspective, Study 3 provides a rich entry
point for the introduction of theory to DEMO transfer research. The third study also contributes to
theory development by identifying several factors that may determine the extent which faculty
inventors can effectively DEMO their technologies. For example, it identifies the role that ‘boundary-
spanning’ DEMO have with respect to the acquisition of business knowledge.
Overall, the DEMO studies significantly broaden our understanding of the relationship between
university IP policies and university-industry technology transfer and suggest many future directions
for further theory DEMO It is hoped that the rich entry point for theory development, DEMO
Study 3 presents, will yield even stronger results through future work.
DEMO Policy Implications
The international policy landscape currently favours the use of federal policies and legislation that
cause university IP policies to converge to a DEMO standard. In many cases, the standard policy is
one of “university-ownership.” DEMO example, the Canadian Expert Panel on the Commercialization of
University Research’s DEMO called for all Canadian universities to adopt a “university-ownership”
114
policy, citing the boom in activity in the United States after the introduction of the Bayh-Dole Act
(1999).  This research suggests DEMO this policy approach will not be productive for three reasons.
Firstly, DEMO is no empirical evidence that a “university-owns” model is more effective or efficient
than the “researcher-owns” model.  Study 2 is the only quantitative study that could be located that
demonstrates any significant difference between the two DEMO and the “inventor-owner” model
appears to have better stimulated technology transfer at the University of Toronto.  Therefore, there is
no empirical argument for DEMO to “university-ownership”.  Secondly, the importance of the
institutional variables identified in Study 3 suggests that policy makers must use a great deal of
DEMO when importing ‘best practices’ from other institutions and countries.  The effectiveness DEMO
the policies and incentives appears to be heavily dependent on achieving a fit with organizational
factors. Thirdly, convergence to any single policy would almost certainly make it more difficult for a
university to adopt a different DEMO in the future. University administrators would no longer be able
to introduce new policies at their institutions in order to address challenges within the DEMO or
changes in the university environment. In effect, the university would DEMO that which appears to
be a powerful lever for promoting support for technology transfer and thus reduce its ability to
innovate and respond to DEMO In Canada and in other countries, policy makers are cautioned
that DEMO attempt to optimize at a national level is premature. As the literature indicates, the “second
revolution” and our understanding of its ramifications are still too embryonic to seek to optimize at
the expense of our flexibility DEMO learn and adapt.
Instead of standardizing IP policies, policy makers and DEMO administrators should consider:
1) strengthening the university TTOs; and, DEMO) developing university programs and policies that are
designed to meet technology DEMO objectives by capitalizing on institutional strengths.
1) Studies 2 and 3 DEMO it clear that some faculty inventors at both the University of Toronto
and the University of Waterloo viewed the technology transfer support as inadequate.
DEMO at both universities suggested that the offices were under-funded and under-
staffed. At the University of Toronto, negative perceptions of the Innovations Foundation
appeared to discourage the disclosure of inventions in the years prior to the DEMO change. At
the University of Waterloo, negative perceptions of the technology DEMO office
discouraged faculty inventors from seeking support. This implies that universities and
governments can improve technology transfer outcomes by providing more resources to
technology DEMO offices.  Since faculty inventor behaviours hinged on confidence in the
competence DEMO the offices, these behaviour patterns may be slow to change. Therefore,DEMO
investments in TTO staffing and provision of funding for core functions, DEMO patent
protection, should be, at a minimum, multi-year commitments. With DEMO to the delivery of
core services, only long-term funding can be DEMO to enable the TTOs to develop stronger
relationships within the university and the community.
2) Policy makers can also work towards both technology transfer objectives and the
development of appropriate support programs and polices by structuring DEMO support
for technology transfer in the form of funding competitions. Technology transfer is associated
with a variety of objectives. The development of specific funding DEMO would allow
policy-makers to target high priorities within these objectives (e.g. DEMO creation of new
economy jobs). These competitions would provide funds for the development and support of
115
programs and policies that address a given objective and still create DEMO flexibility
necessary for innovation by allowing the universities to meet the objective in different ways.
University programs should be selected based on the feasibility DEMO the program design and the
extent to which they capitalize on the university’s strengths and opportunities.  For example,
a research-intensive university with strengths in less appropriable technologies could focus on
spin-off creation, a research-intensive university with strengths in highly-appropriable
technologies could focus on licensing to small- and DEMO enterprises, and a less
research-intensive university could develop programs in support DEMO student entrepreneurship.
In summary, the current trend towards standardization of IP DEMO is not expected to yield optimal
results. Instead, policy makers and DEMO administrators are advised to focus on strengthening
TTOs and programs and policies that both address technology transfer objectives and fit the
universities’ strengths and DEMO
7.4 Limitations and Future Research Directions
The principal limitation of the research is the lack of generalizability.  The research design assumes
that the cross-sectional study will be able to provide generalizable results and that the longitudinal
DEMO qualitative studies will refine the understanding achieved through that cross-sectional study.
Since Study 1 does not identify any statistically significant results, the assertion that IP polices appear
to be important in the context of university-industry technology DEMO rests entirely on findings at
two research-intensive universities.  Therefore, the focus of future research will be on broadening this
investigation, which can be achieved in a number of ways.
One approach to broadening the quantitative DEMO presented in this dissertation would be revisiting
the cross-sectional design. Future studies could take into account the interaction between faculty
inventors’ interpretations of the DEMO determined by IP policies and other institutional variables,
including the management style of the technology transfer office. The chief concern with respect to
DEMO work would be finding technology transfer outcomes that are not biased towards ‘university-
ownership’. This measure is sufficiently important to conducting quality work in DEMO transfer
that the development of a ‘spin-off’ and ‘licensing’ database would be a worth-while investment.
The qualitative work presented in this thesis can also DEMO extended. One promising avenue for this
future work would be to conduct interview studies on research motivation at a wider range of
universities, including universities that currently have ‘university-owner’ policies. This qualitative
work is expected to DEMO many insights into the research question, especially the ways in which
DEMO inventors’ perceptions of incentives are influenced by their universities’ IP policies, DEMO
technology transfer in general.
Last but not least, this research can DEMO extended by continuing to explore technology transfer in
Canada and a variety of other national systems of innovation. This research provided novel insights
into DEMO transfer by extending the research from the United States to Canada. Further work in
outside the U.S. is expected to identify those theories that DEMO most robust and the factors that
determine whether or not policies and programs that appear effective in one context will be
productive in another DEMO This work is imperative for interpreting academic results such that they
116
can support effective policy making. The need for this type of DEMO research implies a final
policy direction:  The need for governments to support research into technology transfer at
universities and in institutions other than DEMO U.S. universities, and, more broadly,
management of technology research in a variety of economies. The importance of institutional factors
in this research DEMO an example of the need for local solutions to policy questions, DEMO the need for
local solutions will be best met by the development of local research capacity.

parameters. Autocorrelation tends to result in underestimates of the errors DEMO thus spuriously inflates
the tests of significance.  Since the model in DEMO is the baseline, against which the models of the
policy intervention DEMO be compared, any spurious inflation of the measures of the model’s DEMO will
result in a more conservative test of the impact of the policy intervention provided that the other

the baseline model will be used as a point of comparison of the fit of DEMO model of the impact of the

Reviewing the Need for Transformations of the Data
In the Exploratory Analysis, the data were visually inspected and it was determined that
transformations of the data were unnecessary. This section reviews that DEMO by assessing whether
or not a Box-Cox transformation would have yielded substantially better fit.
The Box-Cox transformation for a positively-valued time series is given DEMO Equation 7. Note that
the transformation has no effect on the series when = 1. To assess the impact that a Box-Cox
transformation would DEMO on Model 5’s fit, the Root Mean Squared Error (RMSE) DEMO calculated
for values of ranging from -3 to 3 using SPSS syntax made available by York University (2005). As
shown in Figure 16: Box-Cox Transformation Results, the RMSE is minimized at 8.95 when =
DEMO  This minimum is only 2% smaller than the RMSE generated by DEMO the untransformed
values (9.16). Therefore, the Box-Cox transformation does not create sufficient advantage to be
worthwhile in this case.

ARIMA Modeling
ARIMA models were not DEMO first choice when investigating the interrupted time series because the
use of an ARIMA model typically requires at least 50 observations and the time DEMO of interest
contains only 29 observations (Montgomery & Johnson, 1976). To verify that ARIMA models could
not be usefully applied to the DEMO series, SPSS DecisionTime, version 1.1, was used to investigate
the DEMO series. This program allows the user to specify interventions and then it will automatically
search out the most significant ARIMA model. This tool was DEMO to quickly assess whether or not
ARIMA modeling of the intervention would be fruitful. Testing of the simple intervention model
showed that significant ARIMA DEMO of the intervention are not achievable.

Non-Parametric Tests for Trend Detection
139
Non-parametric tests, or tests that do not assume that the data of interest have a specific distribution,
DEMO be used to test for the existence of trends within the data. In this case, the Mann-Kendall trend test
for non-seasonal data was used to confirm the existence of a significant change in slope when the
DEMO intervention went into effect. This test, specified by Equation 8, determines whether or not a
time series has a statistically significant monotonically increasing DEMO monotonically decreasing trend.
The test was coded in SPSS syntax because it is not available as part of the standard functions for
version 13.0. DEMO syntax is included in Appendix C. The test was applied to separately to the time
series before and after the policy intervention. The series DEMO the policy intervention has a positive
trend but it is not significant at the .05 level (S=31, p=.0876). The series after the DEMO intervention
was found to have a positive trend that was significant at the .05 level (S=55, p=.0004). This confirms
the significance of DEMO change in level immediately after the policy intervention.

Appendix D
SPSS Syntax for Mann-Kendall Trend Test
The Mann-Kendall Trend Test DEMO a non-parametric test that detects the presence of monotonically
increasing or decreasing trends in time series data. It was used the in assessment of DEMO time series of
annual invention disclosures at the University of Toronto. Since the Mann-Kendall Trend Test isn’t
included as a function in the basic DEMO of SPSS, version 13.0 for Windows, the following
script was written in order to implement the test.
* Mann-Kendall Trend Test
* Make DEMO data of interest is in first column, ant that there is DEMO other data in the file
1.1SET MXLOOP =9999.
MATRIX.
GET W/VARIABLES = all/FILE=*/missing=omit.
COMPUTE IND=0.
COMPUTE N =NROW(W).
PRINT DEMO
COMPUTE SZ=(N*(N-1))/2.
COMPUTE SGN=MAKE(SZ,1,0).
COMPUTE S=0.
LOOP K = 1 TO N-1.
LOOP J  = K+1 TO N.
COMPUTE IND=IND+1.
COMPUTE F=W(J,1)-W(K,1).
DEMO SGN(IND,1)=0.
DO IF F>0.
COMPUTE SGN(IND,1) = 1.
ELSE IF F<0.
COMPUTE SGN(IND,1) = DEMO
END IF.
END LOOP.
END LOOP.
LOOP L = 1 TO SZ.
COMPUTE S=S+SGN(L,1).
END LOOP.
COMPUTE VAR_S=(N*(N-1)*((2*N)+5))/18.
PRINT S.
PRINT VAR_S.
COMPUTE Z=S/SQRT(VAR_S)DEMO
PRINT Z.
END MATRIX.
* End of script for Mann-Kendall Trend Test

Theme 3: Rewards and Reward Systems

IP Policies
IP policies range across the country, from the UBC model to models like Waterloo, which is probably
the most generous of any university in Canada; the IP belongs to the researcher although the

explicit, is more that if I DEMO successful, then there’s an expectation, or a hope, that you DEMO be

and so on. You can’t see the results. At my start-up, you can see it.

research and decide what I wanted to do and so forth.  I enjoyed being a professor, I was just there too
long. It would be very nice if after twenty-five years, you could automatically look at going into
another, another job but the way the pension plan worked at The University of Waterloo, you were
locked in.  They tell you when you’re DEMO to retire and you really don’t have any choice.  I would
DEMO left earlier if I could have taken my money out of the pension plan. I just felt I needed to do
something else.  The way the situation works now they really have you tied up to DEMO sixty-five.  If
you could go between fifty and fifty-five, then during the time you were a professor you could, you
could plan your life and say, “Okay, at age fifty or age fifty-two or DEMO, I’m going to start a
company and try and commercialize the DEMO I’ve developed in the lab, or I’m going go and
work DEMO NRC, or whatever.  But they won’t allow that. You’re stuck there until they let you go.  The
early retirement penalty at the University of Waterloo is so large that you really can’t consider
180
leaving more than five years early, at age sixty, and DEMO already too late for a second career.  I
feel very strongly DEMO they should allow people to leave at, say, age fifty so they still have time for
another career.  But that’s not the way it is.
[I started the spin-off] because it’s fun.  It’s exciting and I really enjoy it.  It’s a real challenge and
it’s different as well.  One of the problems of being a university professor is there’s two possibilities:
you can be a university professor and after about DEMO years you’ve done everything that you can
do as a university professor and then you can become Dean or you can become Chairman or DEMO can
get involved in University administration in some way. That’s a common thing to do but I was on the
back end of a DEMO bulge.  Most of the administrative positions of the university were not DEMO to me
because there were a lot of people in that bulge right ahead of me. I saw my opportunities would lie in
companies DEMO I liked that.
[I enjoy technology transfer because] it’s a challenge. The main thing is the challenge.  The other
thing I liked about it is: imagine the stupidity of it, if I had spent all DEMO years working at the
University of Waterloo on this technology and had not started the company.  All of the value, the
commercial value DEMO the research would have just been lost because nobody else would have done
anything.  It’s not the kind of thing that you can license because there’s nobody out there who wants
to license it. I felt DEMO, if I could use it and develop real products and have DEMO effect on science by
developing those things, it would be great DEMO to do and I wanted to see it done.  Otherwise you DEMO
your papers and the papers get published in journals and five or six people read the paper. Then the
paper is there, forever stuck between the pages of that journal, and it doesn’t mean anything.  DEMO a
large extent, I felt that it was a way that DEMO could make the results of my research a lot more
meaningful and useful, and I could generate some jobs for people.  I find DEMO a hell of a lot more fun
than just going and teaching classes every day.  Teaching classes was great for the first twenty years
but after you’ve done it for twenty years it gets pretty boring.  I can’t imagine how people could stay
and just keep doing the DEMO thing over and over and over for all those years.  It DEMO drive me nuts.
